<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_flag_waving.svg"><img alt="Yellow flag waving.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="249" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/15px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/22px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/30px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" width="15"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Liberalism" title="Portal:Liberalism">Liberalism portal</a></li>
<li><span style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Free trade</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_policy" title="Trade policy">trade policy</a> that does not restrict <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imports" title="Imports">imports</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exports" title="Exports">exports</a>; it is the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> as applied to <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>. In government, free trade is predominately advocated by political parties that hold <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> economic positions, while economically <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing</a> political parties generally support <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> the opposite of free trade.
</p><p>Most nations are today members of the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> (WTO) <a href="/wiki/Multilateralism" title="Multilateralism">multilateral</a> trade agreements. Free trade is additionally exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mercosur" title="Mercosur">Mercosur</a>, which have established <a href="/wiki/Open_market" title="Open market">open markets</a>. However, most governments still impose some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protectionist" title="Protectionist">protectionist</a> policies that are intended to support local employment, such as applying <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> to imports or <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">subsidies</a> to exports. Governments may also restrict free trade to limit exports of natural resources. Other barriers that may hinder trade include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Import_quotas" title="Import quotas">import quotas</a>, taxes, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-tariff_barrier" title="Non-tariff barrier">non-tariff barriers</a>, such as regulatory <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a>.
</p><p>There is a broad consensus among economists that protectionism has a negative effect on economic growth and economic welfare, while free trade and the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Trade_barrier" title="Trade barrier">trade barriers</a> has a positive effect on economic growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_P_1994_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-See_P_1994-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGMFreeTrade_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGMFreeTrade-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberalization_of_trade" title="Liberalization of trade">liberalization of trade</a> can cause significant and unequally distributed losses, and the economic dislocation of workers in import-competing sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGMFreeTrade_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-IGMFreeTrade-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Free trade policies generally promote the following features:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Trade of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" title="Good (economics)">goods</a> without taxes (including tariffs) or other <a href="/wiki/Trade_barrier" title="Trade barrier">trade barriers</a> (e.g., quotas on imports or subsidies for producers)</li>
<li>Trade in services without taxes or other trade barriers</li>
<li>The absence of "trade-distorting" policies (such as taxes, subsidies, <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulations</a>, or laws) that give some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_entity" title="Business entity">firms</a>, households, or <a href="/wiki/Factors_of_production" title="Factors of production">factors of production</a> an advantage over others</li>
<li>Unregulated access to <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">markets</a></li>
<li>Unregulated access to market information</li>
<li>Inability of firms to distort markets through government-imposed <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oligopoly" title="Oligopoly">oligopoly</a> power</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trade_agreement" title="Trade agreement">Trade agreements</a> which encourage free trade.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_models">Economic models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Economic models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">Supply and demand</a></div>
<p>Two simple ways to understand the proposed benefits of free trade are through David Ricardo's theory of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a> and by analyzing the impact of a tariff or import quota. An economic analysis using the law of supply and demand and the economic effects of a tax can be used to show the theoretical benefits and disadvantages of free trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landsburg_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-landsburg-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartmann_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartmann-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most economists would recommend that even developing nations should set their tariff rates quite low, but the economist <a href="/wiki/Ha-Joon_Chang" title="Ha-Joon Chang">Ha-Joon Chang</a>, a proponent of industrial policy, believes higher levels may be justified in developing nations because the productivity gap between them and developed nations today is much higher than what developed nations faced when they were at a similar level of technological development. Underdeveloped nations today, Chang believes, are weak players in a much more competitive system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated311_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated311-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Counterarguments to Chang's point of view are that the developing countries are able to adopt technologies from abroad, whereas developed nations had to create new technologies themselves, and that developing countries can sell to export markets far richer than any that existed in the 19th century.
</p><p>If the chief justification for a tariff is to stimulate infant industries, it must be high enough to allow domestic manufactured goods to compete with imported goods in order to be successful. This theory, known as <a href="/wiki/Import_substitution_industrialization" title="Import substitution industrialization">import substitution industrialization</a>, is largely considered ineffective for currently developing nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated311_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated311-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tariffs">Tariffs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tariffs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EffectOfTariff.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="600" height="465" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/EffectOfTariff.svg/450px-EffectOfTariff.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/EffectOfTariff.svg/675px-EffectOfTariff.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/EffectOfTariff.svg/900px-EffectOfTariff.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EffectOfTariff.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pink regions are the net loss to society caused by the existence of the tariff.</div></div></div>
<p>The chart at the right analyzes the effect of the imposition of an import tariff on some imaginary good. Prior to the tariff, the price of the good in the world market (and hence in the domestic market) is P<sub>world</sub>. The tariff increases the domestic price to P<sub>tariff</sub>. The higher price causes domestic production to increase from Q<sub>S1</sub> to Q<sub>S2</sub> and causes domestic consumption to decline from Q<sub>C1</sub> to Q<sub>C2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockman_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-stockman-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mankiw_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mankiw-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>This has three main effects on societal welfare. Consumers are made worse off because the consumer surplus (green region) becomes smaller. Producers are better off because the producer surplus (yellow region) is made larger. The government also has additional tax revenue (blue region). However, the loss to consumers is greater than the gains by producers and the government. The magnitude of this societal loss is shown by the two pink triangles. Removing the tariff and having free trade would be a net gain for society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockman_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-stockman-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mankiw_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mankiw-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>An almost identical analysis of this tariff from the perspective of a net producing country yields parallel results. From that country's perspective, the tariff leaves producers worse off and consumers better off, but the net loss to producers is larger than the benefit to consumers (there is no tax revenue in this case because the country being analyzed is not collecting the tariff). Under similar analysis, export tariffs, import quotas, and export quotas all yield nearly identical results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landsburg_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-landsburg-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes consumers are better off and producers worse off, and sometimes consumers are worse off and producers are better off, but the imposition of trade restrictions causes a net loss to society because the losses from trade restrictions are larger than the gains from trade restrictions. Free trade creates winners and losers, but theory and empirical evidence show that the size of the winnings from free trade are larger than the losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landsburg_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-landsburg-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_diversion">Trade diversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Trade diversion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_economics" title="Mainstream economics">mainstream economic</a> theory, the selective application of free trade agreements to some countries and tariffs on others can lead to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Efficiency_(economics)" title="Efficiency (economics)">economic inefficiency</a> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Trade_diversion" title="Trade diversion">trade diversion</a>. It is economically efficient for a good to be produced by the country which is the lowest cost producer, but this does not always take place if a high cost producer has a free trade agreement while the low cost producer faces a high tariff. Applying free trade to the high cost producer (and not the low cost producer as well) can lead to trade diversion and a net economic loss. This is why many economists place such high importance on negotiations for global tariff reductions, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doha_Round" title="Doha Round">Doha Round</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landsburg_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-landsburg-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economist_opinions">Economist opinions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Economist opinions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The literature analysing the economics of free trade is extremely rich with extensive work having been done on the theoretical and empirical effects. Though it creates winners and losers, the broad consensus among economists is that free trade is a large and unambiguous net gain for society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In a 2006 survey of American economists (83 responders), "87.5% agree that the U.S. should eliminate remaining tariffs and other barriers to trade" and "90.1% disagree with the suggestion that the U.S. should restrict employers from outsourcing work to foreign countries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Quoting Harvard economics professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N._Gregory_Mankiw" title="N. Gregory Mankiw">N. Gregory Mankiw</a>, "Few propositions command as much consensus among professional economists as that open world trade increases economic growth and raises living standards."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In a survey of leading economists, none disagreed with the notion that "freer trade improves productive efficiency and offers consumers better choices, and in the long run these gains are much larger than any effects on employment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most economists would agree<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that although increasing returns to scale might mean that certain industry could settle in a geographical area without any strong economic reason derived from comparative advantage, this is not a reason to argue against free trade because the absolute level of output enjoyed by both "winner" and "loser" will increase with the "winner" gaining more than the "loser" but both gaining more than before in an absolute level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_international_trade" title="Timeline of international trade">Timeline of international trade</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_era">Early era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Corn_Law_League" title="Anti-Corn Law League">Anti-Corn Law League</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_ricardo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="363" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/David_ricardo.jpg/170px-David_ricardo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/David_ricardo.jpg/255px-David_ricardo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/David_ricardo.jpg/340px-David_ricardo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_ricardo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Ricardo</div></div></div>
<p>The notion of a free trade system encompassing multiple sovereign states originated in a rudimentary form in 16th century <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Imperial Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrighi1994_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arrighi1994-22">[22]</a></sup> American <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurist</a> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Nussbaum" title="Arthur Nussbaum">Arthur Nussbaum</a> noted that Spanish theologian <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a> was "the first to set forth the notions (though not the terms) of freedom of commerce and freedom of the seas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nussbaum1947_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nussbaum1947-23">[23]</a></sup> Vitoria made the case under principles of <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_gentium" title="Jus gentium">jus gentium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nussbaum1947_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nussbaum1947-23">[23]</a></sup> However, it was two early British economists <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a> who later developed the idea of free trade into its modern and recognizable form.
</p><p>Economists who advocated free trade believed trade was the reason why certain civilizations prospered economically. Adam Smith, for example, pointed to increased trading as being the reason for the flourishing of not just <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean</a> cultures such as <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Rome</a>, but also of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> (<a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">East India</a>) and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The great prosperity of the Netherlands after throwing off Spanish Imperial rule and pursuing a policy of free trade<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Appleby2010_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Appleby2010-24">[24]</a></sup> made the free trade/mercantilist dispute the most important question in economics for centuries. Free trade policies have battled with <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a>, and other policies over the centuries.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> had <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">liberal</a> free trade policies by the 18th century, with origins in <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Capitulations of the Ottoman Empire">capitulations of the Ottoman Empire</a>, dating back to the first commercial treaties signed with France in 1536 and taken further with <a href="/wiki/Capitulation_(treaty)" title="Capitulation (treaty)">capitulations</a> in 1673, in 1740 which lowered <a href="/wiki/Duty_(economics)" title="Duty (economics)">duties</a> to only 3% for imports and exports, and in 1790. Ottoman free trade policies were praised by British economists advocating free trade, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._R._McCulloch" title="J. R. McCulloch">J. R. McCulloch</a> in his <i>Dictionary of Commerce</i> (1834), but criticized by British politicians opposing free trade, such as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">prime minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, who cited the Ottoman Empire as "an instance of the injury done by unrestrained competition" in the 1846 <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> debate, arguing that it destroyed what had been "some of the finest manufactures of the world" in 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Droits_de_douane_(France,_UK,_US).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="1015" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png/220px-Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png/330px-Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png/440px-Droits_de_douane_%28France%2C_UK%2C_US%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Droits_de_douane_(France,_UK,_US).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Average Tariff Rates (France, UK, US)</div></div></div>
<p>Trade in <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">colonial America</a> was regulated by the British mercantile system through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acts_of_Trade_and_Navigation" title="Acts of Trade and Navigation">Acts of Trade and Navigation</a>. Until the 1760s, few colonists openly advocated for free trade, in part because regulations were not strictly enforced – New England was famous for smuggling – but also because colonial merchants did not want to compete with foreign goods and shipping. According to historian Oliver Dickerson, a desire for free trade was not one of the causes of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. "The idea that the basic mercantile practices of the eighteenth century were wrong," wrote Dickerson, "was not a part of the thinking of the Revolutionary leaders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Free trade came to what would become the United States as a result of <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. After the British Parliament issued the <a href="/wiki/Prohibitory_Act" title="Prohibitory Act">Prohibitory Act</a>, blockading colonial ports, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> responded by effectively declaring economic independence, opening American ports to foreign trade on April 6, 1776. According to historian John W. Tyler, "Free trade had been forced on the Americans, like it or not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 1801 the Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pius_VII" title="Pius VII">Pius VII</a> ordered some liberalization of trade to face the economic crisis in the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motu_Proprio" title="Motu Proprio">Motu Proprio</a> "Le più colte"; despite of this, the export of national corn was forbidden to ensure the food for the State.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1383" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/220px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/330px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/440px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britain waged two <a href="/wiki/Opium_Wars" title="Opium Wars">Opium Wars</a> to force China to legalize the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opium_trade" title="Opium trade">opium trade</a> and to open all of China to British merchants.</div></div></div>
<p>In Britain, free trade became a central principle <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom#Free_trade" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">practiced by the repeal of the Corn Laws in 1846</a>.   Large-scale agitation was sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Corn_Law_League" title="Anti-Corn Law League">Anti-Corn Law League</a>.  Under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a>, China opened five <a href="/wiki/Treaty_ports" title="Treaty ports">treaty ports</a> to world trade in 1843. The first free trade agreement, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobden-Chevalier_Treaty" title="Cobden-Chevalier Treaty">Cobden-Chevalier Treaty</a>, was put in place in 1860 between Britain and France, which led to successive agreements between other countries in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many classical liberals, especially in 19th and early 20th century Britain (e.g. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>) and in the United States for much of the 20th century (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> and Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Cordell_Hull" title="Cordell Hull">Cordell Hull</a>), believed that free trade promoted peace.  <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> included free-trade rhetoric in his "<a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a>" speech of 1918:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The program of the world's peace, therefore, is our program; and that program, the only possible program, all we see it, is this: [...]
</p><p>3. The removal, so far as possible, of all economic barriers and the establishment of equality of trade conditions among all the nations consenting to the peace and associating themselves for its maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote><p>
According to economic historian Douglas Irwin, a common myth about U.S. trade policy is that low tariffs harmed American manufacturers in the early 19th century and then that high tariffs made the United States into a great industrial power in the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-30">[30]</a></sup> A review by the <i>Economist</i> of Irwin's 2017 book <i>Clashing over Commerce: A History of US Trade Policy</i> notes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-30">[30]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>Political dynamics would lead people to see a link between tariffs and the economic cycle that was not there. A boom would generate enough revenue for tariffs to fall, and when the bust came pressure would build to raise them again. By the time that happened, the economy would be recovering, giving the impression that tariff cuts caused the crash and the reverse generated the recovery. Mr Irwin also methodically debunks the idea that protectionism made America a great industrial power, a notion believed by some to offer lessons for developing countries today. As its share of global manufacturing powered from 23% in 1870 to 36% in 1913, the admittedly high tariffs of the time came with a cost, estimated at around 0.5% of GDP in the mid-1870s. In some industries, they might have sped up development by a few years. But American growth during its protectionist period was more to do with its abundant resources and openness to people and ideas.</p></blockquote><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bairoch" title="Paul Bairoch">Paul Bairoch</a>, since the end of the 18th century, the United States has been "the homeland and bastion of modern protectionism". In fact, the United States never adhered to free trade until 1945. For the most part, the "Jeffersonians" strongly opposed it. In the 19th century, statesmen such as Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> continued Hamilton's themes within the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> under the name "<a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a>." The opposition <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> contested several elections throughout the 1830s, 1840s, and 1850s in part over the issue of the tariff and protection of industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the U.S., the Democratic Party favored moderate tariffs used for government revenue only, while the Whigs favored higher protective tariffs to protect favored industries. The economist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Charles_Carey" title="Henry Charles Carey">Henry Charles Carey</a> became a leading proponent of the "American System" of economics. This mercantilist "American System" was opposed by the Democratic Party of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>.
</p><p>The fledgling <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, who called himself a "Henry Clay tariff Whig", strongly opposed free trade and implemented a 44% tariff during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> – in part to pay for railroad subsidies and for the war effort, and to protect favored industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> (later to become President of the United States) stated the stance of the Republican Party (which won every election for President from 1868 until 1912, except the two non-consecutive terms of <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>) as thus:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Under free trade the trader is the master and the producer the slave. Protection is but the law of nature, the law of self-preservation, of self-development, of securing the highest and best destiny of the race of man. [It is said] that protection is immoral…. Why, if protection builds up and elevates 63,000,000 [the U.S. population] of people, the influence of those 63,000,000 of people elevates the rest of the world. We cannot take a step in the pathway of progress without benefitting mankind everywhere. Well, they say, 'Buy where you can buy the cheapest'…. Of course, that applies to labor as to everything else. Let me give you a maxim that is a thousand times better than that, and it is the protection maxim: 'Buy where you can pay the easiest.' And that spot of earth is where labor wins its highest rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>During the interwar period, economic <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a> took hold in the United States, most famously in the form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoot-Hawley_Tariff_Act" title="Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act">Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act</a>, which is credited by economists with the prolonging and worldwide propagation of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eun_&amp;_Resnick_2011_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eun_&amp;_Resnick_2011-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>33</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> From 1934, trade liberalization began to take place through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reciprocal_Trade_Agreements_Act" title="Reciprocal Trade Agreements Act">Reciprocal Trade Agreements Act</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="514" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg/220px-20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg/330px-20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg/440px-20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20041120-1_bushchinamtg-1-515h.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hu_Jintao" title="Hu Jintao">Hu Jintao</a> of China meet while attending an <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">APEC</a> summit in Santiago de Chile, 2004.</div></div></div>
<p>In <i>Kicking Away the Ladder</i>, development economist <a href="/wiki/Ha-Joon_Chang" title="Ha-Joon Chang">Ha-Joon Chang</a> reviews the history of free trade policies and economic growth, and notes that many of the now-industrialized countries had significant barriers to trade throughout their history. The United States and Britain, sometimes considered the homes of free trade policy, employed protectionism to varying degrees at all times. Britain abolished the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>, which restricted import of grain, in 1846 in response to domestic pressures, and it reduced protectionism for manufactures in the mid 19th century, when its technological advantage was at its height, but tariffs on manufactured products had returned to 23% by 1950. The United States maintained weighted average tariffs on manufactured products of approximately 40–50% up until the 1950s, augmented by the natural protectionism of high transportation costs in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The most consistent practitioners of free trade have been Switzerland, the Netherlands, and to a lesser degree Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Chang describes the <a href="/wiki/Export-oriented_industrialization" title="Export-oriented industrialization">export-oriented industrialization</a> policies of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tigers</a> as "far more sophisticated and fine-tuned than their historical equivalents".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some degree of protectionism is nevertheless the norm throughout the world. Most developed nations maintain controversial<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> agricultural tariffs. From 1820 to 1980, the average tariffs on manufactures in twelve industrial countries ranged from 11 to 32%. In the developing world, average tariffs on manufactured goods are approximately 34%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The American economist <a href="/wiki/C._Fred_Bergsten" title="C. Fred Bergsten">C. Fred Bergsten</a> devised the ‘bicycle theory’ to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_policy" title="Trade policy">trade policy</a>. According to this model trade policy is ‘dynamically unstable’, in that it constantly tends towards either liberalisation or protectionism. To prevent falling off the bike (the disadvantages of protectionism), trade policy and <a href="/wiki/Multilateral_trade_negotiations" title="Multilateral trade negotiations">multilateral trade negotiations</a> must constantly pedal towards greater liberalisation. To achieve greater liberalisation decision makers must appeal to the greater welfare for consumers and the wider national economy over narrower parochial interests. However, Bergsten also posits that it is also necessary to compensate the losers in trade and help them find new work, as this will both reduce the backlash against globalisation and the motives for trades unions and politicians to call for protection of trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II">Post-World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, in part due to industrial size and the onset of the Cold War, the United States has often been a proponent of reduced tariff-barriers and free trade. The U.S. helped establish the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a> (GATT) and later the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> (WTO); although it had rejected an earlier version in the 1950s (<a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Organization" title="International Trade Organization">International Trade Organization</a> or ITO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Since the 1970s, U.S. governments have negotiated managed-trade agreements, such as the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> (NAFTA) in the 1990s, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic-Central_America_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Dominican Republic-Central America Free Trade Agreement">Dominican Republic-Central America Free Trade Agreement</a> (CAFTA) in 2006, and a number of bilateral agreements (such as with Jordan).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Europe, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Six" title="Inner Six">six countries</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/European_Coal_and_Steel_Community" title="European Coal and Steel Community">European Coal and Steel Community</a> (ECSC) in 1951 which became the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC) in 1958. Two core objectives of the EEC were the development of a common market, subsequently renamed the <a href="/wiki/Single_market" title="Single market">single market</a>, and establishing a <a href="/wiki/Customs_union" title="Customs union">customs union</a> between its member states. After expanding its membership, the EEC became the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) in 1993. The European Union, now the world's largest single market,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> has <a href="/wiki/European_Union_free_trade_agreements" title="European Union free trade agreements">concluded free trade agreements</a> with many countries around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2830" data-file-width="4254" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg/250px-1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg/375px-1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg/500px-1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1_singapore_city_skyline_dusk_panorama_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> is the top country in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Enabling_Trade_Report" title="Global Enabling Trade Report">Enabling Trade Index</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_multilateral_free_trade_agreements" title="List of multilateral free trade agreements">List of multilateral free trade agreements</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_bilateral_free_trade_agreements" title="List of bilateral free trade agreements">List of bilateral free trade agreements</a></div>
<p>Most countries in the world are members of the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> which limits in certain ways but does not eliminate tariffs and other trade barriers. Most countries are also members of regional free trade areas that lower trade barriers among participating countries. The EU and the US are negotiating a <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_Trade_and_Investment_Partnership" title="Transatlantic Trade and Investment Partnership">Transatlantic Trade and Investment Partnership</a>. Initially led by the U.S., twelve countries that have borders on the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> are currently in private negotiations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> around the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Pacific_Partnership" title="Trans-Pacific Partnership">Trans-Pacific Partnership</a>, which is being touted by the negotiating countries as a free trade policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In January 2017, the United States pulled out of negotiations for the Trans-Pacific Parternship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Degree_of_free_trade_policies">Degree of free trade policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Degree of free trade policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Free trade may apply to trade in  <a href="/wiki/Goods_and_services" title="Goods and services">services as well as in goods</a>. Non-economic considerations may inhibit free trade: a country may espouse free trade in principle but (for example) ban certain drugs (such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a>) or certain practices (such as <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>),
thus limiting international free trade.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_trade_in_goods">Free trade in goods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Free trade in goods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_Enabling_Trade_Report" title="Global Enabling Trade Report">Global Enabling Trade Report</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Global_Enabling_Trade_Report" title="Global Enabling Trade Report">Enabling Trade Index</a> measures the factors, policies and services that facilitate the trade in goods across borders and to destinations. The index summarizes four sub-indexes: market access; border administration; transport and communications infrastructure; and business environment. The top 30 countries and areas as of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">

<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1600" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/23px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/35px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/46px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> 5.6</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1800" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> 5.6</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/23px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/35px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/45px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> 5.5</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The relative <a href="/wiki/Social_cost" title="Social cost">costs</a>, benefits and beneficiaries of free trade are debated by academics, governments and interest groups.
</p><p>Arguments for protectionism fall into the economic category (trade hurts the economy or groups in the economy) or the moral category (the effects of trade might help the economy, but have ill effects in other areas); a general argument against free trade is that it is <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> in disguise. The moral category is wide, including concerns of destroying infant industries and undermining long-run economic development, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Income_inequality" title="Income inequality">income inequality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>, supporting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">child labor</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweatshops" title="Sweatshops">sweatshops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_bottom" title="Race to the bottom">race to the bottom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage slavery</a>, accentuating poverty in poor countries, harming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defense_(military)" title="Defense (military)">national defense</a>, and forcing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_change" title="Cultural change">cultural change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Economic arguments against free trade criticize the assumptions or conclusions of economic theories. Sociopolitical arguments against free trade cite social and political effects that economic arguments do not capture, such as political stability, national security, human rights and environmental protection.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Free trade is often opposed by domestic industries that would have their profits and market share reduced by lower prices for imported goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brakman_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-brakman-52">[52]</a></sup> For example, if United States tariffs on imported sugar were reduced, U.S. sugar producers would receive lower prices and profits, while U.S. sugar consumers would spend less for the same amount of sugar because of those same lower prices. The economic theory of <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a> holds that consumers would necessarily gain more than producers would lose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pugel_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-pugel-54">[54]</a></sup> Since each of those few domestic sugar producers would lose a lot while each of a great number of consumers would gain only a little, domestic producers are more likely to mobilize against the lifting of tariffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brakman_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-brakman-52">[52]</a></sup> More generally, producers often favor domestic subsidies and tariffs on imports in their home countries, while objecting to subsidies and tariffs in their export markets.
</p>
<div style="float:right;text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg" title="Real Wages vs Trade as a Percent of GDP"><img alt="Real Wages vs Trade as a Percent of GDP" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="824" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg/500px-Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg/750px-Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg/1000px-Real_Wages_vs_Trade_Percent_of_GDP.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a><br/><i>Real Wages vs Trade as a Percent of GDP<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </i></div>
<p>Socialists frequently oppose free trade on the ground that it allows maximum <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour" title="Exploitation of labour">exploitation</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labor_movement" title="Labor movement">workers</a> by <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Communist Manifesto</a></i>, "The bourgeoisie... has set up that single, unconscionable freedom – free trade. In one word, for exploitation, veiled by religious and political illusions, it has substituted naked, shameless, direct, brutal exploitation." Nonetheless, Marx did favor free trade – <i>solely</i> because he felt that it would hasten the social revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Free trade" is opposed by many anti-globalization groups, based on their assertion that free trade agreements generally do not increase the <a href="/wiki/Economic_freedom" title="Economic freedom">economic freedom</a> of the poor or the working class, and frequently make them poorer. Where the foreign supplier allows de facto exploitation of labor, domestic free-labor is unfairly forced to compete with the foreign exploited labor. To this extent, free trade is seen as an end-run around workers' rights and laws that protect individual liberty.
</p><p>Some opponents of free trade favor free trade theory but oppose free trade <i>agreements</i> as applied. Some opponents of NAFTA see the agreement as being materially harmful to the common people, but some of the arguments are actually against the particulars of government-managed trade, rather than against free trade <i>per se</i>. For example, it is argued that it would be wrong to let subsidized corn from the U.S. into Mexico freely under NAFTA at prices well below production cost (<a href="/wiki/Dumping_(pricing_policy)" title="Dumping (pricing policy)">dumping</a>) because of its ruinous effects to Mexican farmers. Of course, such subsidies violate free trade theory, so this argument is not actually against the principle of free trade, but rather its selective implementation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Research shows that support for trade restrictions is highest among respondents with the lowest levels of education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup> The authors find "that the impact of education on how voters' think about trade and globalization has more to do with exposure to economic ideas and information about the aggregate and varied effects of these economic phenomena, than it does with individual calculations about how trade affects personal income or job security. This is not to say that the latter types of calculations are not important in shaping individuals' views of trade – just that they are not being manifest in the simple association between education and support for trade openness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup> A 2017 study found that individuals whose occupations are routine-task-intensive and who do jobs that are offshorable are more likely to be protectionist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research suggests that attitudes towards free trade do not necessarily reflect individuals' self-interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonialism">Colonialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Colonialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dependency_theory" title="Dependency theory">Dependency theory</a></div>
<p>It has long been argued that free trade is a form of colonialism or imperialism, a position taken by various proponents of <a href="/wiki/Economic_nationalism" title="Economic nationalism">economic nationalism</a> and the school of mercantilism. In the 19th century these criticized British calls for free trade as cover for <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, notably in the works of American <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, architect of the <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German American</a> economist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_List" title="Friedrich List">Friedrich List</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>More recently, Ecuadorian President <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Correa" title="Rafael Correa">Rafael Correa</a> has denounced the "sophistry of free trade" in an introduction he wrote for a book titled <i>The Hidden Face of Free Trade Accords</i>, written in part by Correa's current Energy Minister Alberto Acosta. Citing as his source the book <a class="external text" href="http://www.paecon.net/PAEtexts/Chang1.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>Kicking Away the Ladder</i></a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Ha-Joon_Chang" title="Ha-Joon Chang">Ha-Joon Chang</a>, Correa identified the difference between an "American system" opposed to a "British System" of free trade. The latter, he says, was explicitly viewed by the Americans as "part of the British imperialist system." According to Correa, Chang showed that it was Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, and not <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_List" title="Friedrich List">Friedrich List</a>, who was the first to present a systematic argument defending industrial protectionism.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives">Alternatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Alternatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The following alternatives for free trade have been proposed: <a href="/wiki/Balanced_trade" title="Balanced trade">balanced trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_policy" title="Industrial policy">industrial policy</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature">In literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The value of free trade was first observed and documented by <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, in 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> He wrote,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is the maxim of every prudent master of a family, never to attempt to make at home what it will cost him more to make than to buy.... If a foreign country can supply us with a commodity cheaper than we ourselves can make it, better buy it of them with some part of the produce of our own industry, employed in a way in which we have some advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>This statement uses the concept of <a href="/wiki/Absolute_advantage" title="Absolute advantage">absolute advantage</a> to present an argument in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a>, the dominant view surrounding trade at the time, which held that a country should aim to export more than it imports, and thus amass wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Instead, Smith argues, countries could gain from each producing exclusively the good(s) in which they are most suited to, trading between each other as required for the purposes of consumption. In this vein, it is not the value of exports relative to that of imports that is important, but the value of the goods produced by a nation. The concept of absolute advantage however does not address a situation where a country has no advantage in the production of a particular good or type of good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>This theoretical shortcoming was addressed by the theory of comparative advantage. Generally attributed to <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a> who expanded on it in his 1817 book <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Principles_of_Political_Economy_and_Taxation" title="On the Principles of Political Economy and Taxation">On the Principles of Political Economy and Taxation</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> it makes a case for free trade based not on absolute advantage in production of a good, but on the relative <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">opportunity costs</a> of production. A country should specialize in whatever good it can produce at the lowest cost, trading this good to buy other goods it requires for consumption. This allows for countries to benefit from trade even when they do not have an absolute advantage in any area of production. While their gains from trade might not be equal to those of a country more productive in all goods, they will still be better off economically from trade than they would be under a state of <a href="/wiki/Autarky" title="Autarky">autarky</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exceptionally, <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>'s 1886 book <i><a href="/wiki/Protection_or_Free_Trade" title="Protection or Free Trade">Protection or Free Trade</a></i> was read out loud in full into the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Record" title="Congressional Record">Congressional Record</a> by five Democratic congressmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Tyler_Cowen" title="Tyler Cowen">Tyler Cowen</a> wrote that '<a href="/wiki/Protection_or_Free_Trade" title="Protection or Free Trade">Protection or Free Trade</a><i> "remains perhaps the best-argued tract on free trade to this day."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></i>
George discusses the subject in particular with respect to the interests of labor. Although George is very critical towards protectionism:
</p>
<blockquote><p>We all hear with interest and pleasure of improvements in transportation by water or land; we are all disposed to regard the opening of canals, the building of railways, the deepening of harbors, the improvement of steamships as beneficial. But if such things are beneficial, how can tariffs be beneficial? The effect of such things is to lessen the cost of transporting commodities; the effect of tariffs is to increase it. If the protective theory be true, every improvement that cheapens the carriage of goods between country and country is an injury to mankind unless tariffs be commensurately increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> George considers the general free trade argument 'inadequate'. He argues that the removal of protective tariffs alone is never sufficient to improve the situation of the working class, unless accompanied by a shift towards <a href="/wiki/Land_value_tax" title="Land value tax">land value tax</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/28px-Emblem-money.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/42px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/56px-Emblem-money.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Business_and_economics" title="Portal:Business and economics">Business and economics portal</a></span></li></ul></div>

<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Gentlemen deceive themselves. It is not free trade that they are recommending to our acceptance. It is, in effect, the British colonial system that we are invited to adopt; and, if their policy prevail, it will lead, substantially, to the recolonization of these States, under the commercial dominion of Great Britain.", "In Defense of the American System, Against the British Colonial System." 1832, Feb 2, 3, and 6, <cite class="citation journal">Clay, Henry (1843). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Biyh3OmxhOMC&amp;pg=PA23" rel="nofollow">"The Life and Speeches of Henry Clay"</a>. <b>II</b>: 23–24.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=The+Life+and+Speeches+of+Henry+Clay&amp;rft.volume=II&amp;rft.pages=23-24&amp;rft.date=1843&amp;rft.aulast=Clay&amp;rft.aufirst=Henry&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DBiyh3OmxhOMC%26pg%3DPA23&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFree+trade"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Had the English left everything to itself – '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laissez_faire" title="Laissez faire">Laissez faire</a>, laissez aller', as the popular economical school recommends – the [German] merchants of the <a href="/wiki/Steelyard" title="Steelyard">Steelyard</a> would be still carrying on their trade in London, the Belgians would be still manufacturing cloth for the English, England would have still continued to be the sheep-farm of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hansards</a>, just as Portugal became the vineyard of England, and has remained so till our days, owing to the stratagem of a cunning diplomatist."</span>
